### Exercises

#### Exercise 1

Consider a navigation system that uses three sensors to estimate the position of a vehicle. The sensors have measurement errors with standard deviations of 0.5 meters, 0.3 meters, and 0.2 meters in the x, y, and z directions, respectively. Calculate the variance-covariance matrix for this system.



#### Exercise 2

Suppose we have a navigation system that uses a Kalman filter to estimate the position of a vehicle. The filter has a state vector with three elements representing the x, y, and z coordinates. The process noise covariance matrix is given by $Q = \begin{bmatrix} 0.1 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 0.2 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & 0.3 \end{bmatrix}$. Calculate the variance-covariance matrix for the state vector after one time step.



#### Exercise 3

A navigation system uses a GPS receiver to estimate the position of a vehicle. The receiver has a measurement error with a standard deviation of 5 meters in the horizontal direction and 10 meters in the vertical direction. If the receiver takes 10 measurements per second, what is the variance-covariance matrix for the position estimate after 1 second?



#### Exercise 4

Suppose we have a navigation system that uses a Kalman filter to estimate the position of a vehicle. The filter has a state vector with four elements representing the x, y, z coordinates and velocity. The measurement model is given by $z = Hx + v$, where $H = \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 0 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 & 0 & 0 \end{bmatrix}$ and $v$ is the measurement noise with a standard deviation of 0.1 meters. Calculate the variance-covariance matrix for the state vector after one time step.



#### Exercise 5

Consider a navigation system that uses a single sensor to estimate the position of a vehicle. The sensor has a measurement error with a standard deviation of 0.5 meters in both the x and y directions. If the sensor takes 100 measurements per second, what is the variance-covariance matrix for the position estimate after 1 second? How does this compare to the variance-covariance matrix for a system with the same sensor but taking only 10 measurements per second?





## Chapter: Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



### Introduction:



In today's world, navigation has become an essential part of our daily lives. From finding our way to a new destination to tracking our fitness activities, we rely heavily on navigation systems. One of the most widely used and advanced navigation systems is the Global Positioning System (GPS). In this chapter, we will delve into the details of GPS pseudo-range measurements and the basic principles behind them.



GPS is a satellite-based navigation system that provides accurate positioning, navigation, and timing information to users all over the world. It consists of a network of 24 satellites orbiting the Earth, constantly transmitting signals that can be received by GPS receivers on the ground. These receivers use the signals from multiple satellites to determine their precise location, velocity, and time.



The concept of pseudo-range measurements is crucial to understanding how GPS works. Pseudo-range is the apparent distance between a GPS receiver and a satellite, calculated using the time it takes for the signal to travel from the satellite to the receiver. This measurement is affected by various factors such as atmospheric delays, receiver clock errors, and satellite clock errors. Understanding these factors is essential in accurately interpreting GPS measurements.



In this chapter, we will explore the basic principles behind GPS pseudo-range measurements and how they are used to determine a receiver's position. We will also discuss the various sources of error that can affect these measurements and how they can be mitigated. By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of GPS pseudo-range measurements and their role in modern navigation systems.





# GPS signals



### Navigation message



In addition to the PRN ranging codes, a receiver needs to know the time and position of each active satellite. GPS encodes this information into the "navigation message" and modulates it onto both the C/A and P(Y) ranging codes at 50 bit/s. The navigation message format described in this section is called LNAV data (for "legacy navigation").



The navigation message conveys information of three types:



- Ephemeris: This is the precise orbit and clock information for each satellite, valid for only four hours. The receiver uses this information to calculate the satellite's position at any given time.

- Almanac: This is a less precise orbit and clock information for all satellites, valid for up to two weeks. The receiver uses this information to acquire a set of satellites based on stored time and location. As each satellite is acquired, its ephemeris is decoded so the satellite can be used for navigation.

- Health: This information indicates the status of each satellite, including any potential issues or malfunctions.



The navigation message consists of 30-second "frames" 1,500 bits long, divided into five 6-second "subframes" of ten 30-bit words each. Each subframe has the GPS time in 6-second increments. Subframe 1 contains the GPS date (week number) and satellite clock correction information, satellite status and health. Subframes 2 and 3 together contain the transmitting satellite's ephemeris data. Subframes 4 and 5 contain "page" 1 through 25 of the 25-page almanac. The almanac is 15,000 bits long and takes 12.5 minutes to transmit.



A frame begins at the start of the GPS week and every 30 seconds thereafter. Each week begins with the transmission of almanac page 1.



There are two navigation message types: LNAV-L is used by satellites with PRN numbers 1 to 32 (called "lower PRN numbers") and LNAV-U is used by satellites with PRN numbers 33 to 63 (called "upper PRN numbers"). The two types use very similar formats. Subframes 1 to 3 are the same while subframes 4 and 5 are almost the same. Each message type contains almanac data for all satellites using the same navigation message type, but not the other.



Each subframe begins with a Telemetry Word (TLM) that enables the receiver to detect the beginning of a subframe and determine the type of data being transmitted. This is crucial for the receiver to properly decode the navigation message.



### Section: 6.1 GPS Signal Tracking:



GPS signal tracking is the process by which a receiver acquires and tracks the signals from multiple satellites to determine its position. This process involves several steps, including signal acquisition, tracking, and measurement.



#### Subsection: 6.1a GPS Signal Structure



To understand how GPS signal tracking works, it is important to first understand the structure of a GPS signal. The GPS signal is composed of two codes: the Coarse/Acquisition (C/A) code and the Precise (P) code. The C/A code is a 1.023 MHz pseudo-random noise (PRN) code that is used for civilian purposes, while the P code is a 10.23 MHz PRN code that is used for military purposes.



The C/A code is modulated onto the L1 carrier frequency of 1575.42 MHz, while the P code is modulated onto both the L1 and L2 carrier frequencies of 1575.42 MHz and 1227.60 MHz, respectively. This dual-frequency modulation allows for more accurate measurements and error correction.



The GPS signal is transmitted in a series of bursts, with each burst containing a navigation message and a ranging code. The navigation message is modulated onto both the C/A and P codes, while the ranging code is unique to each satellite and is used for ranging and timing purposes.



To track a GPS signal, the receiver must first acquire the signal by detecting the presence of the C/A code. Once the signal is acquired, the receiver can then track the signal by continuously measuring the phase and frequency of the C/A code. This allows the receiver to determine the pseudo-range between the satellite and the receiver.



In addition to the C/A code, the receiver also tracks the P code to obtain more precise measurements. However, since the P code is encrypted for military use, civilian receivers can only track the encrypted P code, known as the P(Y) code. This code is modulated onto the L2 carrier frequency and is used for more accurate measurements and error correction.



In the next section, we will discuss the basic principles behind GPS pseudo-range measurements and how they are used to determine a receiver's position.





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.1: GPS Signal Tracking



In order for a GPS receiver to accurately determine its position, it must be able to track and acquire signals from multiple satellites. This process, known as signal tracking, is essential for the proper functioning of the GPS system.



#### Subsection 6.1b: Signal Acquisition and Tracking



Signal acquisition and tracking is the process by which a GPS receiver locks onto and maintains a connection with a satellite signal. This is achieved through a series of steps, including signal acquisition, code tracking, and carrier tracking.



##### Signal Acquisition



The first step in signal acquisition is to search for and acquire the satellite signal. This is done by scanning a range of frequencies and correlating the received signal with a known code sequence. Once a correlation is found, the receiver can lock onto the signal and begin tracking it.



##### Code Tracking



Once the signal has been acquired, the receiver must track the code sequence being transmitted by the satellite. This is done by continuously correlating the received signal with the known code sequence. Any discrepancies between the received signal and the code sequence can be used to calculate the time delay between the receiver and the satellite, which is essential for determining the receiver's position.



##### Carrier Tracking



In addition to tracking the code sequence, the receiver must also track the carrier frequency being transmitted by the satellite. This is done by continuously measuring the phase and frequency of the received signal and adjusting the receiver's local oscillator to match the carrier frequency. This allows the receiver to accurately measure the time delay between the receiver and the satellite, which is used to calculate the receiver's position.

 1 # Extended Kalman filter



## Generalizations



### Continuous-time extended Kalman filter



Model

\dot{\mathbf{x}}(t) &= f\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t), \mathbf{u}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{w}(t) &\mathbf{w}(t) &\sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{Q}(t)\bigr) \\

\mathbf{z}(t) &= h\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{v}(t) &\mathbf{v}(t) &\sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{R}(t)\bigr)

</math>

Initialize

\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t_0)=E\bigl[\mathbf{x}(t_0)\bigr] \text{, } \mathbf{P}(t_0)=Var\bigl[\mathbf{x}(t_0)\bigr]

</math>

Predict-Update

\dot{\hat{\mathbf{x}}}(t) &= f\bigl(\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t),\mathbf{u}(t)\bigr)+\mathbf{K}(t)\Bigl(\mathbf{z}(t)-h\bigl(\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t)\bigr)\Bigr)\\

\dot{\mathbf{P}}(t) &= \mathbf{F}(t)\mathbf{P}(t)+\mathbf{P}(t)\mathbf{F}(t)^{T}-\mathbf{K}(t)\mathbf{H}(t)\mathbf{P}(t)+\mathbf{Q}(t)\\

\mathbf{K}(t) &= \mathbf{P}(t)\mathbf{H}(t)^{T}\mathbf{R}(t)^{-1}\\

\mathbf{F}(t) &= \left . \frac{\partial f}{\partial \mathbf{x} } \right \vert _{\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t),\mathbf{u}(t)}\\

\mathbf{H}(t) &= \left . \frac{\partial h}{\partial \mathbf{x} } \right \vert _{\hat{\mathbf{x}}(t)} 

</math>

Unlike the discrete-time extended Kalman filter, the prediction and update steps are coupled in the continuous-time extended Kalman filter.



#### Discrete-time measurements



Most physical systems are represented as continuous-time models while discrete-time measurements are frequently taken for state estimation via a digital processor. Therefore, the system model and measurement model are given by

\dot{\mathbf{x}}(t) &= f\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t), \mathbf{u}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{w}(t) &\mathbf{w}(t) &\sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{Q}(t)\bigr) \\

\mathbf{z}_k &= h(\mathbf{x}_k) + \mathbf{v}_k &\mathbf{v}_k &\sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{R}_k\bigr)

</math>

Initialize

\hat{\mathbf{x}}_0=E\bigl[\mathbf{x}_0\bigr] \text{, } \mathbf{P}_0=Var\bigl[\mathbf{x}_0\bigr]

</math>

Predict-Update

\hat{\mathbf{x}}_{k+1} &= f\bigl(\hat{\mathbf{x}}_k,\mathbf{u}_k\bigr)\\

\mathbf{P}_{k+1} &= \mathbf{F}_k\mathbf{P}_k\mathbf{F}_k^T+\mathbf{Q}_k\\

\mathbf{K}_k &= \mathbf{P}_k\mathbf{H}_k^T\bigl(\mathbf{H}_k\mathbf{P}_k\mathbf{H}_k^T+\mathbf{R}_k\bigr)^{-1}\\

\hat{\mathbf{x}}_{k+1} &= \hat{\mathbf{x}}_k+\mathbf{K}_k\bigl(\mathbf{z}_k-h(\hat{\mathbf{x}}_k)\bigr)\\

\mathbf{P}_{k+1} &= \bigl(\mathbf{I}-\mathbf{K}_k\mathbf{H}_k\bigr)\mathbf{P}_k

</math>

The discrete-time extended Kalman filter is a recursive algorithm that estimates the state of a nonlinear system using a linearized model and a series of measurements. It is widely used in navigation and control systems due to its simplicity and effectiveness.

# GPS signals



### Navigation message



In addition to the PRN ranging codes, a receiver needs to know the time and position of each active satellite. GPS encodes this information into the "navigation message" and modulates it onto both the C/A and P(Y) ranging codes at 50 bit/s. - Almanac: This is a less precise orbit and clock information for all satellites, valid for up to two weeks. The receiver uses this information to acquire a set of satellites based on stored time and location. As each satellite is acquired, its ephemeris is decoded so the satellite can be used for navigation.

The two types use very similar formats. Subframes 1, 2, and 3 contain the ephemeris data for the transmitting satellite, while subframes 4 and 5 contain the almanac data. This information is essential for the receiver to accurately determine its position.



#### Generalizations



The continuous-time extended Kalman filter is a powerful tool for estimating the state of a nonlinear system using a linearized model and a series of measurements. It is widely used in navigation and control systems due to its simplicity and effectiveness. However, it is important to note that the prediction and update steps are coupled in this filter, which can lead to errors if the system is highly nonlinear.



In contrast, the discrete-time extended Kalman filter is a recursive algorithm that estimates the state of a nonlinear system using a linearized model and a series of measurements. It is widely used in navigation and control systems due to its simplicity and effectiveness. However, it is important to note that the prediction and update steps are decoupled in this filter, which can lead to errors if the system is highly nonlinear.



#### Discrete-time measurements



Most physical systems are represented as continuous-time models while discrete-time measurements are frequently taken for state estimation via a digital processor. Therefore, the system model and measurement model are given by

\dot{\mathbf{x}}(t) &= f\bigl(\mathbf{x}(t), \mathbf{u}(t)\bigr) + \mathbf{w}(t) &\mathbf{w}(t) &\sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{Q}(t)\bigr) \\

\mathbf{z}_k &= h(\mathbf{x}_k) + \mathbf{v}_k &\mathbf{v}_k &\sim \mathcal{N}\bigl(\mathbf{0},\mathbf{R}_k\bigr)

</math>

Initialize

\hat{\mathbf{x}}_0=E\bigl[\mathbf{x}_0\bigr] \text{, } \mathbf{P}_0=Var\bigl[\mathbf{x}_0\bigr]

</math>

Predict-Update

\hat{\mathbf{x}}_{k+1} &= f\bigl(\hat{\mathbf{x}}_k,\mathbf{u}_k\bigr)\\

\mathbf{P}_{k+1} &= \mathbf{F}_k\mathbf{P}_k\mathbf{F}_k^T+\mathbf{Q}_k\\

\mathbf{K}_k &= \mathbf{P}_k\mathbf{H}_k





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.1: GPS Signal Tracking



In order for a GPS receiver to accurately determine its position, it must be able to track and acquire signals from multiple satellites. This process, known as signal tracking, is essential for the proper functioning of the GPS system.



#### Subsection 6.1c: Effects of Multipath and Interference



Signal tracking can be affected by various factors, such as multipath and interference. Multipath refers to the phenomenon where the GPS signal bounces off of objects, such as buildings or trees, before reaching the receiver. This can result in the receiver receiving multiple signals from the same satellite, with varying time delays. This can cause errors in the receiver's calculations, leading to inaccurate position estimates.



Interference, on the other hand, refers to the presence of other signals in the same frequency band as the GPS signal. This can be caused by other electronic devices, such as Wi-Fi routers, operating in the same area. Interference can disrupt the receiver's ability to accurately track and acquire the GPS signal, leading to errors in position calculations.



To mitigate the effects of multipath and interference, GPS receivers use various techniques such as signal filtering and error correction algorithms. These techniques help to reduce the impact of multipath and interference on the receiver's calculations, resulting in more accurate position estimates.



### Related Context

```

# Urban canyon



### GPS signal reception



When using GPS receivers in street canyons with tall buildings, the shadowing and multipath effects may contribute to poor GPS signal reception # Wi-Fi



### Radio propagation



With Wi-Fi signals line-of-sight usually works best, but signals can transmit, absorb, reflect, refract, diffract and up and down fade through and around structures, both man-made and natural. Wi-Fi signals are very strongly affected by metallic structures (including rebar in concrete, low-e coatings in glazing) and water (such as found in vegetation.)



Due to the complex nature of radio propagation at typical Wi-Fi frequencies, particularly around trees and buildings, algorithms can only approximately predict Wi-Fi signal strength for any given area in relation to a transmitter. This effect does not apply equally to long-range Wi-Fi, since longer links typically operate from towers that transmit above the surrounding foliage.



Mobile use of Wi-Fi over wider ranges is limited, for instance, to uses such as in an automobile moving from one hotspot to another. Other wireless technologies are more suitable for communicating with moving vehicles.



#### Distance records



Distance records (using non-standard devices) include in June 2007, held by Ermanno Pietrosemoli and EsLaRed of Venezuela, transferring about 3 MB of data between the mountain-tops of El Águila and Platillon. The Swedish Space Agency transferred data , using 6 watt amplifiers to reach an overhead stratospheric balloon.



### Interference



Wi-Fi connections can be blocked or the Internet speed lowered by having other devices in the same area. Wi-Fi protocols are designed to share the wavebands reasonably fairly, and this often works with little to no disruption. To minimize collisions with Wi-Fi and non-Wi-Fi devices, Wi-Fi employs Carrier-sense multiple access with collision avoidance (CSMA/CA), where transmitters listen before transmitting and delay transmission of packets if they detect th

```



The effects of multipath and interference can be particularly pronounced in urban environments, where tall buildings and other structures can create what is known as an "urban canyon." In these areas, the GPS signal can be blocked or reflected by the surrounding buildings, resulting in a weaker and more distorted signal reaching the receiver.



This can be further compounded by the presence of other electronic devices, such as Wi-Fi routers, which operate in the same frequency band as the GPS signal. These devices can cause interference, making it more difficult for the receiver to accurately track and acquire the GPS signal.



To overcome these challenges, GPS receivers use advanced signal processing techniques to filter out unwanted signals and correct for any errors caused by multipath and interference. These techniques have greatly improved the reliability and accuracy of GPS systems, making them an essential tool for modern navigation.

## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.1: GPS Signal Tracking



In order for a GPS receiver to accurately determine its position, it must be able to track and acquire signals from multiple satellites. This process, known as signal tracking, is essential for the proper functioning of the GPS system.



#### Subsection 6.1b: Signal Acquisition and Tracking



Signal acquisition and tracking is the process by which a GPS receiver locks onto and maintains a connection with a satellite signal. This is achieved through a series of steps, including signal acquisition, code tracking, and carrier tracking.



##### Signal Acquisition



The first step in signal acquisition is to search for and acquire the satellite signal. This is done by scanning a range of frequencies and correlating the received signal with a known code sequence. Once a correlation is found, the receiver can lock onto the signal and begin tracking it.



##### Code Tracking



Once the signal has been acquired, the receiver must track the code sequence being transmitted by the satellite. This is done by continuously correlating the received signal with the known code sequence. Any discrepancies between the received signal and the code sequence can be used to calculate the time delay between the receiver and the satellite, which is essential for determining the receiver's position.



##### Carrier Tracking



In addition to tracking the code sequence, the receiver must also track the carrier frequency being transmitted by the satellite. This is done by continuously measuring the phase and frequency of the received signal and adjusting the receiver's local oscillator to match the carrier frequency. This allows the receiver to accurately measure the time delay between the receiver and the satellite, which is used to calculate the receiver's position.



#### Subsection 6.1c: Effects of Multipath and Interference



Signal tracking can be affected by various factors, such as multipath and interference. Multipath refers to the phenomenon where the GPS signal bounces off of objects, such as buildings or trees, before reaching the receiver. This can result in the receiver receiving multiple signals from the same satellite, with varying time delays. This can cause errors in the receiver's calculations, leading to inaccurate position estimates.



Interference, on the other hand, refers to the presence of other signals in the same frequency band as the GPS signal. This can be caused by other electronic devices, such as Wi-Fi routers, operating in the same area. Interference can disrupt the receiver's ability to accurately track and acquire the GPS signal, leading to errors in position calculations.



To mitigate the effects of multipath and interference, GPS receivers use various techniques such as signal filtering and error correction algorithms. These techniques help to reduce the impact of multipath and interference on the receiver's calculations, resulting in more accurate position estimates.





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.2: Orbit Geometry



In order for the GPS system to accurately determine a receiver's position, it relies on precise knowledge of the orbits of the GPS satellites. This section will discuss the basic principles of orbit geometry and how it relates to GPS satellite orbits.



#### Subsection 6.2a: GPS Satellite Orbits



The GPS satellite constellation consists of 24 satellites, each orbiting the Earth at an altitude of approximately 20,200 km. These satellites follow a specific type of orbit known as a Medium Earth Orbit (MEO). This type of orbit is chosen for GPS satellites because it provides a good balance between coverage and accuracy.



The MEO orbit is a circular orbit with an inclination of 55 degrees to the equator. This inclination allows the satellites to cover the entire Earth's surface, with the exception of the polar regions. The satellites are also evenly spaced out in their orbits, with four satellites in each of six orbital planes. This arrangement ensures that at least four satellites are always visible from any point on Earth, allowing for accurate position calculations.



The GPS satellites also have a specific orbital period of 12 hours, meaning they complete one full orbit around the Earth in 12 hours. This period is carefully chosen to ensure that the satellites are always in the same position relative to the Earth's surface, making it easier for receivers to track and acquire their signals.

The GPS satellite orbits are carefully designed and maintained to ensure the accuracy and reliability of the GPS system. The Indian Regional Navigation Satellite System (IRNSS) and other satellite systems, such as Kepler-7b, Kepler-16b, and Kepler-9c, also have their own unique orbital parameters and configurations. However, the principles of orbit geometry and the importance of precise orbital knowledge remain the same for all satellite systems.



The LS I +61 303 satellite, also known as SOLRAD 7A, is an example of a satellite that is still in orbit and can be tracked online. This highlights the importance of maintaining accurate orbital information for satellites, as it allows for their continued use and operation.



Other satellites, such as those listed in the List of Fast Rotators (minor planets), have completed their missions and are no longer in orbit. This serves as a reminder of the limited lifespan of satellites and the need for continuous updates and replacements in order to maintain a functioning satellite system.



In conclusion, understanding the principles of orbit geometry is crucial for the proper functioning of satellite systems, including GPS. Accurate knowledge and maintenance of satellite orbits is essential for the success and reliability of these systems.





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.2: Orbit Geometry



In order for the GPS system to accurately determine a receiver's position, it relies on precise knowledge of the orbits of the GPS satellites. This section will discuss the basic principles of orbit geometry and how it relates to GPS satellite orbits.



#### Subsection 6.2a: GPS Satellite Orbits



The GPS satellite constellation consists of 24 satellites, each orbiting the Earth at an altitude of approximately 20,200 km. These satellites follow a specific type of orbit known as a Medium Earth Orbit (MEO). This type of orbit is chosen for GPS satellites because it provides a good balance between coverage and accuracy.



The MEO orbit is a circular orbit with an inclination of 55 degrees to the equator. This inclination allows the satellites to cover the entire Earth's surface, with the exception of the polar regions. The satellites are also evenly spaced out in their orbits, with four satellites in each of six orbital planes. This arrangement ensures that at least four satellites are always visible from any point on Earth, allowing for accurate position calculations.



The GPS satellites also have a specific orbital period of 12 hours, meaning they complete one full orbit around the Earth in 12 hours. This period is carefully chosen to ensure that the satellites are always in the same position relative to the Earth's surface, making it easier for receivers to track and acquire their signals.



#### Subsection 6.2b: Satellite Visibility and Dilution of Precision



In order for a GPS receiver to accurately determine its position, it must be able to receive signals from at least four satellites. However, the number of visible satellites at any given time can vary due to factors such as satellite position, receiver location, and atmospheric conditions. This is known as satellite visibility.



Satellite visibility is an important factor in determining the accuracy of GPS measurements. When there are fewer satellites in view, the receiver's position calculation may be less accurate due to a phenomenon known as Dilution of Precision (DOP). DOP is a measure of the geometric relationship between the satellites in view and the receiver's position. A higher DOP value indicates a less precise position calculation.



To mitigate the effects of DOP, GPS receivers use a technique called selective availability (SA). SA involves intentionally degrading the accuracy of GPS signals for civilian users, in order to prevent potential adversaries from using the system for military purposes. However, in May 2000, the US government discontinued the use of SA, resulting in a significant increase in the accuracy of GPS measurements for civilian users.



In addition to satellite visibility and SA, other factors such as atmospheric conditions and receiver errors can also affect the accuracy of GPS measurements. Therefore, it is important for GPS users to understand the principles of orbit geometry and satellite visibility in order to obtain the most accurate position calculations.





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.2: Orbit Geometry



In order for the GPS system to accurately determine a receiver's position, it relies on precise knowledge of the orbits of the GPS satellites. This section will discuss the basic principles of orbit geometry and how it relates to GPS satellite orbits.



#### Subsection 6.2a: GPS Satellite Orbits



The GPS satellite constellation consists of 24 satellites, each orbiting the Earth at an altitude of approximately 20,200 km. These satellites follow a specific type of orbit known as a Medium Earth Orbit (MEO). This type of orbit is chosen for GPS satellites because it provides a good balance between coverage and accuracy.



The MEO orbit is a circular orbit with an inclination of 55 degrees to the equator. This inclination allows the satellites to cover the entire Earth's surface, with the exception of the polar regions. The satellites are also evenly spaced out in their orbits, with four satellites in each of six orbital planes. This arrangement ensures that at least four satellites are always visible from any point on Earth, allowing for accurate position calculations.



The GPS satellites also have a specific orbital period of 12 hours, meaning they complete one full orbit around the Earth in 12 hours. This period is carefully chosen to ensure that the satellites are always in the same position relative to the Earth's surface, making it easier for receivers to track and acquire their signals.



#### Subsection 6.2b: Satellite Visibility and Dilution of Precision



In order for a GPS receiver to accurately determine its position, it must be able to receive signals from at least four satellites. However, the number of visible satellites at any given time can vary due to factors such as satellite position, receiver location, and atmospheric conditions. This is known as satellite visibility.



Satellite visibility is an important factor in determining the accuracy of GPS measurements. The more satellites that are visible, the more accurate the position calculation will be. However, the geometry of the satellites also plays a role in the accuracy of GPS measurements. This is known as Dilution of Precision (DOP).



DOP is a measure of the geometric relationship between the satellites and the receiver. It takes into account factors such as the position of the satellites, the angle between them, and the distance between them and the receiver. A lower DOP value indicates a better geometric relationship and therefore a more accurate position calculation.



#### Subsection 6.2c: Effects of Orbit Errors



While the GPS system is designed to have precise knowledge of the satellite orbits, errors can still occur. These errors can be caused by factors such as atmospheric drag, solar radiation pressure, and gravitational perturbations from other celestial bodies.



One of the main effects of orbit errors is a shift in the satellite's position relative to the Earth's surface. This can result in a change in the satellite's elevation angle as seen from the receiver, which can affect the accuracy of the position calculation. Additionally, orbit errors can also lead to a decrease in satellite visibility and an increase in DOP, resulting in a less accurate position calculation.



To mitigate the effects of orbit errors, the GPS system uses a process called orbit determination. This involves continuously monitoring the satellite orbits and making adjustments to correct any errors. This ensures that the GPS system maintains its high level of accuracy and reliability. 





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.3: Specific Signal Structure



In order for GPS receivers to accurately determine their position, they must be able to receive and interpret the signals transmitted by GPS satellites. This section will discuss the specific structure of these signals and how they are used in GPS pseudo-range measurements.



#### Subsection 6.3a: GPS Signal Components



The GPS signal is composed of several different components, each with its own unique purpose. These components include the carrier signal, the navigation message, and the pseudo-random noise (PRN) code.



The carrier signal is a high-frequency radio wave that is transmitted by the GPS satellites. This signal is used to carry the navigation message and the PRN code. The carrier signal has a frequency of 1575.42 MHz, which is in the L1 band of the electromagnetic spectrum.



The navigation message is a data signal that contains information about the GPS satellite's orbit, clock correction, and other important parameters. This message is transmitted at a rate of 50 bits per second and is modulated onto the carrier signal using binary phase shift keying (BPSK).



The PRN code is a unique code that is assigned to each GPS satellite. This code is used to differentiate between the signals transmitted by different satellites. The PRN code is a pseudo-random sequence of ones and zeros that repeats every 1 millisecond. This code is modulated onto the carrier signal using binary phase shift keying (BPSK).



In addition to these main components, the GPS signal also includes several other components that are used for error correction and signal integrity. These include the C/A code, the P code, and the M code. These codes are used to improve the accuracy and security of the GPS signal.



The C/A code (coarse/acquisition code) is a short PRN code that is used for civilian GPS applications. It has a chip rate of 1.023 MHz and is transmitted on the L1 band. This code is used for initial acquisition and tracking of the GPS signal.



The P code (precision code) is a longer PRN code that is used for military GPS applications. It has a chip rate of 10.23 MHz and is transmitted on both the L1 and L2 bands. This code is more complex and difficult to decode, providing a higher level of security for military use.



The M code (military code) is a new code that is being introduced in the latest generation of GPS satellites. It has a chip rate of 10.23 MHz and is transmitted on the L1, L2, and L5 bands. This code is designed to provide even higher levels of security and accuracy for military use.



In summary, the GPS signal is composed of several different components that work together to provide accurate and reliable positioning information. These components include the carrier signal, the navigation message, and the PRN code, as well as additional codes for error correction and security. Understanding the structure and function of these components is essential for understanding how GPS pseudo-range measurements are made.





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.3: Specific Signal Structure



In order for GPS receivers to accurately determine their position, they must be able to receive and interpret the signals transmitted by GPS satellites. This section will discuss the specific structure of these signals and how they are used in GPS pseudo-range measurements.



#### Subsection 6.3b: Pseudorandom Noise Codes



Pseudorandom noise (PRN) codes play a crucial role in the functioning of GPS systems. These codes are used to differentiate between the signals transmitted by different satellites, allowing receivers to accurately determine their position.



The PRN codes used in GPS systems are generated by a pre-output function, which consists of two registers of size 128 bits: the non-linear feedback shift register (NLFSR) and the linear feedback shift register (LFSR). These registers are updated using feedback polynomials and a boolean function, resulting in a pseudo-random sequence of ones and zeros.



The feedback polynomials used in the pre-output function are carefully chosen to ensure that the resulting PRN codes have desirable properties such as long periods and good correlation properties. For example, the feedback polynomial for the NLFSR is <math>f(x)=1+x^{32}+x^{47}+x^{58}+x^{90}+x^{121}+x^{128}</math>, while the feedback polynomial for the LFSR is <math>g(x)=1+x^{32}+x^{37}+x^{72}+x^{102}+x^{128}+x^{44}x^{60}+x^{61}x^{125}+x^{63}x^{67}x^{69}x^{101}+x^{80}x^{88}+x^{110}x^{111}+x^{115}x^{117}+x^{46}x^{50}x^{58}+x^{103}x^{104}x^{106}+x^{33}x^{35}x^{36}x^{40}</math>.



The boolean function used in the pre-output function is <math>h(x)=b_{i+12}s_{i+8}+s_{i+13}s_{i+20}+b_{i+95}s_{i+42}+s_{i+60}s_{i+79}+b_{i+12}b_{i+95}s_{i+94}</math>, where <math>b_i</math> and <math>s_i</math> represent the bits in the NLFSR and LFSR, respectively.



In addition to the feedback polynomials and boolean function, the NLFSR and LFSR are also updated using update functions. These update functions ensure that the PRN codes have good statistical properties and are resistant to attacks.



The resulting PRN codes are then modulated onto the carrier signal using binary phase shift keying (BPSK). This allows the receiver to extract the PRN code from the received signal and use it to differentiate between the signals transmitted by different satellites.



In summary, PRN codes are an essential component of GPS systems, allowing receivers to accurately determine their position by differentiating between the signals transmitted by different satellites. These codes are generated using carefully chosen feedback polynomials and a boolean function, and are modulated onto the carrier signal using BPSK. 





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.3: Specific Signal Structure



In order for GPS receivers to accurately determine their position, they must be able to receive and interpret the signals transmitted by GPS satellites. This section will discuss the specific structure of these signals and how they are used in GPS pseudo-range measurements.



#### Subsection 6.3c: Carrier Phase and Doppler Shift



In addition to the pseudorandom noise codes discussed in the previous section, GPS signals also contain carrier waves that are used to determine the receiver's position. These carrier waves have a specific frequency and phase that can be measured by the receiver.



The carrier frequency received by the receiver can vary due to the Doppler shift, which is caused by the relative motion between the satellite and the receiver. This means that the points where received PRN sequences begin may not differ from 0 by an exact integral number of milliseconds. To account for this, the receiver uses carrier frequency tracking along with PRN code tracking to determine when the received satellite's PRN code begins.



Carrier frequency tracking involves observing two quantities: phase error and received frequency offset. The correlation of the received PRN code with respect to the receiver-generated PRN code is computed to determine if the bits of the two signals are misaligned. This allows the receiver to estimate the amount of adjustment required for maximum correlation, which is used to determine the phase error. The received frequency offset from the frequency generated by the receiver provides an estimate of the phase rate error.



The command for the frequency generator and any further PRN code shifting required are then computed as a function of the phase error and the phase rate error in accordance with the control law used. This allows the receiver to accurately track the carrier frequency and maintain lock with the satellite.



In addition to determining the receiver's position, the carrier phase and Doppler shift can also be used to calculate the Doppler velocity. This is the velocity component along the line of sight of the receiver relative to the satellite. By continuously tracking the carrier phase and Doppler shift, the receiver can accurately determine its velocity and position in real-time.



As the receiver continues to read successive PRN sequences, it will encounter a sudden change in the phase of the 1,023-bit received PRN signal. This indicates the beginning of a data bit of the navigation message, allowing the receiver to begin reading the 20 millisecond bits of the navigation message. This enables the receiver to decode the navigation message and obtain important information such as satellite ephemeris data and clock corrections.



In conclusion, the carrier phase and Doppler shift play a crucial role in the functioning of GPS systems. By accurately tracking these parameters, receivers are able to determine their position and velocity with high precision and accuracy. 





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.4: Phase Measurements and Data



In addition to pseudo-range measurements, GPS receivers also use phase measurements to determine their position. This section will discuss the principles behind phase measurements and how they are used in GPS navigation.



#### Subsection 6.4a: GPS Carrier Phase Measurements



GPS carrier phase measurements involve tracking the phase of the carrier wave transmitted by a GPS satellite. This phase can be measured by comparing it to a reference signal generated by the receiver. The difference in phase between the two signals can then be used to determine the distance between the satellite and the receiver.



One challenge in using carrier phase measurements is the Doppler shift caused by the relative motion between the satellite and the receiver. This shift can cause the received frequency to vary from the expected frequency, making it difficult to accurately measure the phase. To account for this, receivers use carrier frequency tracking to estimate the phase error and adjust the reference signal accordingly.



Carrier frequency tracking involves observing two quantities: phase error and received frequency offset. The correlation of the received PRN code with respect to the receiver-generated PRN code is computed to determine if the bits of the two signals are misaligned. This allows the receiver to estimate the amount of adjustment required for maximum correlation, which is used to determine the phase error. The received frequency offset from the frequency generated by the receiver provides an estimate of the phase rate error.



Once the phase error and phase rate error are determined, the receiver can adjust the reference signal to accurately measure the phase difference between the satellite and the receiver. This allows for more precise distance measurements and ultimately improves the accuracy of the receiver's position calculation.



In addition to distance measurements, carrier phase measurements can also be used to calculate the velocity of the receiver. By tracking the changes in phase over time, the receiver can determine the Doppler shift and use it to calculate its velocity relative to the satellite.



Overall, GPS carrier phase measurements play a crucial role in modern navigation systems, providing accurate distance and velocity measurements that are essential for determining a receiver's position. 





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.4: Phase Measurements and Data



In addition to pseudo-range measurements, GPS receivers also use phase measurements to determine their position. This section will discuss the principles behind phase measurements and how they are used in GPS navigation.



#### Subsection 6.4a: GPS Carrier Phase Measurements



GPS carrier phase measurements involve tracking the phase of the carrier wave transmitted by a GPS satellite. This phase can be measured by comparing it to a reference signal generated by the receiver. The difference in phase between the two signals can then be used to determine the distance between the satellite and the receiver.



One challenge in using carrier phase measurements is the Doppler shift caused by the relative motion between the satellite and the receiver. This shift can cause the received frequency to vary from the expected frequency, making it difficult to accurately measure the phase. To account for this, receivers use carrier frequency tracking to estimate the phase error and adjust the reference signal accordingly.



Carrier frequency tracking involves observing two quantities: phase error and received frequency offset. The correlation of the received PRN code with respect to the receiver-generated PRN code is computed to determine if the bits of the two signals are misaligned. This allows the receiver to estimate the amount of adjustment required for maximum correlation, which is used to determine the phase error. The received frequency offset from the frequency generated by the receiver provides an estimate of the phase rate error.



Once the phase error and phase rate error are determined, the receiver can adjust the reference signal to accurately measure the phase difference between the satellite and the receiver. This allows for more precise distance measurements and ultimately improves the accuracy of the receiver's position calculation.



#### Subsection 6.4b: Cycle Slips and Ambiguity Resolution



One issue that can arise in phase measurements is the occurrence of cycle slips. A cycle slip occurs when the phase measurement suddenly jumps by an integer number of cycles, causing a sudden change in the calculated distance between the satellite and the receiver. This can happen due to various factors such as signal interference, receiver malfunction, or loss of lock on the satellite signal.



Cycle slips can significantly affect the accuracy of the receiver's position calculation, as they introduce errors in the measured phase difference. To mitigate this issue, receivers use a process called ambiguity resolution. This process involves identifying and correcting any cycle slips that may have occurred in the phase measurements.



Ambiguity resolution is achieved by comparing the phase measurements from multiple satellites. Since the satellites are transmitting at different frequencies, their phase measurements will have different values. By comparing these values, the receiver can identify any discrepancies and correct them by adjusting the reference signal.



However, ambiguity resolution is not always straightforward. In some cases, the phase measurements from different satellites may have similar values, making it difficult to determine which one is correct. This is known as the "integer ambiguity" problem. To resolve this, receivers use techniques such as integer least squares or integer bootstrapping to determine the correct integer value for the phase measurements.



In conclusion, phase measurements play a crucial role in GPS navigation, providing more precise distance measurements and improving the accuracy of the receiver's position calculation. However, they also come with their own set of challenges, such as cycle slips and ambiguity resolution. By understanding these challenges and implementing appropriate techniques, receivers can overcome them and provide accurate and reliable navigation information.

## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.4: Phase Measurements and Data



In addition to pseudo-range measurements, GPS receivers also use phase measurements to determine their position. This section will discuss the principles behind phase measurements and how they are used in GPS navigation.



#### Subsection 6.4c: Applications in High-Precision Navigation



Phase measurements in GPS navigation have a wide range of applications, particularly in high-precision navigation. One such application is in the field of geodesy, where precise measurements of the Earth's shape and gravitational field are essential for a variety of scientific and engineering purposes.



One example of this is in the determination of the Earth's rotation and polar motion. By tracking the phase of GPS signals from multiple satellites, it is possible to accurately measure the Earth's rotation rate and any changes in its axis of rotation. This information is crucial for understanding the Earth's dynamics and predicting future changes.



Another application of phase measurements is in the field of surveying. With the use of high-precision GPS receivers, surveyors can accurately measure the position of points on the Earth's surface with centimeter-level accuracy. This allows for the creation of highly detailed and accurate maps, as well as the monitoring of changes in the Earth's surface over time.



Phase measurements also play a crucial role in the field of precision agriculture. By using GPS receivers to track the phase of signals from satellites, farmers can precisely map their fields and monitor changes in soil moisture and nutrient levels. This information can then be used to optimize irrigation and fertilizer application, leading to more efficient and sustainable farming practices.



In addition to these applications, phase measurements are also used in high-precision navigation for aircraft and ships. By accurately tracking the phase of GPS signals, pilots and navigators can determine their position with a high degree of accuracy, allowing for safer and more efficient travel.



Overall, the use of phase measurements in GPS navigation has revolutionized the field of high-precision navigation and has enabled a wide range of applications in various industries. As technology continues to advance, it is likely that even more precise and sophisticated uses of phase measurements will be developed, further enhancing our ability to navigate and understand the world around us.





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.5: Pseudorange Measurements



Pseudorange measurements are a fundamental component of GPS navigation. They are used to determine the distance between a GPS receiver and a satellite, which is then used to calculate the receiver's position. In this section, we will discuss the definition and properties of pseudorange measurements.



#### Subsection 6.5a: Definition and Properties of Pseudorange



Pseudorange is defined as the apparent range between a GPS receiver and a satellite, measured in units of time. It is called "pseudo" because it is not the actual physical distance between the two points, but rather an estimate based on the time it takes for the signal to travel from the satellite to the receiver.



Pseudorange measurements are affected by several factors, including atmospheric delays, satellite clock errors, and receiver clock errors. These errors can introduce inaccuracies in the pseudorange measurement, which can affect the accuracy of the receiver's position calculation.



One of the key properties of pseudorange measurements is that they are relative measurements. This means that the pseudorange between a receiver and a satellite is only meaningful when compared to the pseudorange between the same receiver and another satellite. This is because the receiver clock error affects all pseudorange measurements equally, so it cancels out when comparing the pseudorange between two satellites.



Another important property of pseudorange measurements is that they are one-way measurements. This means that the receiver is only measuring the time it takes for the signal to travel from the satellite to the receiver. It does not take into account the time it takes for the signal to travel from the receiver to the satellite. This is because the receiver does not have precise knowledge of its own clock error, so it cannot accurately measure the two-way travel time.



In summary, pseudorange measurements are an essential component of GPS navigation, providing crucial information for determining a receiver's position. However, they are subject to errors and have specific properties that must be taken into account when using them for navigation. In the next section, we will discuss the techniques used to mitigate these errors and improve the accuracy of pseudorange measurements.





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.5: Pseudorange Measurements



Pseudorange measurements are a fundamental component of GPS navigation. They are used to determine the distance between a GPS receiver and a satellite, which is then used to calculate the receiver's position. In this section, we will discuss the definition and properties of pseudorange measurements.



#### Subsection 6.5a: Definition and Properties of Pseudorange



Pseudorange is defined as the apparent range between a GPS receiver and a satellite, measured in units of time. It is called "pseudo" because it is not the actual physical distance between the two points, but rather an estimate based on the time it takes for the signal to travel from the satellite to the receiver.



Pseudorange measurements are affected by several factors, including atmospheric delays, satellite clock errors, and receiver clock errors. These errors can introduce inaccuracies in the pseudorange measurement, which can affect the accuracy of the receiver's position calculation.



One of the key properties of pseudorange measurements is that they are relative measurements. This means that the pseudorange between a receiver and a satellite is only meaningful when compared to the pseudorange between the same receiver and another satellite. This is because the receiver clock error affects all pseudorange measurements equally, so it cancels out when comparing the pseudorange between two satellites.



Another important property of pseudorange measurements is that they are one-way measurements. This means that the receiver is only measuring the time it takes for the signal to travel from the satellite to the receiver. It does not take into account the time it takes for the signal to travel from the receiver to the satellite. This is because the receiver does not have precise knowledge of its own clock error, so it cannot accurately measure the round-trip time of the signal.



### Subsection 6.5b: Pseudorange Errors and Corrections



As mentioned in the previous subsection, pseudorange measurements are affected by various errors that can introduce inaccuracies in the measurement. These errors can be categorized into two types: systematic errors and random errors.



Systematic errors are caused by factors that affect all pseudorange measurements in a similar manner. These include atmospheric delays, satellite clock errors, and receiver clock errors. Atmospheric delays are caused by the ionosphere and troposphere, which can slow down the GPS signal as it travels through them. Satellite clock errors refer to inaccuracies in the satellite's onboard clock, which can affect the timing of the signal. Receiver clock errors, on the other hand, refer to inaccuracies in the receiver's clock, which can also affect the timing of the signal.



Random errors, on the other hand, are caused by factors that affect each pseudorange measurement differently. These include multipath interference, which occurs when the GPS signal reflects off of objects before reaching the receiver, and noise in the receiver's electronics.



To account for these errors, various corrections can be applied to the pseudorange measurements. These corrections include the ionospheric correction, which accounts for the delay caused by the ionosphere, and the satellite clock correction, which accounts for the inaccuracies in the satellite's clock. Additionally, techniques such as differential GPS can be used to eliminate or reduce the effects of multipath interference and noise.



In conclusion, pseudorange measurements are a crucial component of GPS navigation, but they are not without their limitations. By understanding the properties and sources of errors in pseudorange measurements, we can apply corrections and techniques to improve the accuracy of GPS positioning. 





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.5: Pseudorange Measurements



Pseudorange measurements are a fundamental component of GPS navigation. They are used to determine the distance between a GPS receiver and a satellite, which is then used to calculate the receiver's position. In this section, we will discuss the definition and properties of pseudorange measurements.



#### Subsection 6.5a: Definition and Properties of Pseudorange



Pseudorange is defined as the apparent range between a GPS receiver and a satellite, measured in units of time. It is called "pseudo" because it is not the actual physical distance between the two points, but rather an estimate based on the time it takes for the signal to travel from the satellite to the receiver.



Pseudorange measurements are affected by several factors, including atmospheric delays, satellite clock errors, and receiver clock errors. These errors can introduce inaccuracies in the pseudorange measurement, which can affect the accuracy of the receiver's position calculation.



One of the key properties of pseudorange measurements is that they are relative measurements. This means that the pseudorange between a receiver and a satellite is only meaningful when compared to the pseudorange between the same receiver and another satellite. This is because the receiver clock error affects all pseudorange measurements equally, so it cancels out when comparing the pseudorange between two satellites.



Another important property of pseudorange measurements is that they are one-way measurements. This means that the receiver is only measuring the time it takes for the signal to travel from the satellite to the receiver. It does not take into account the time it takes for the signal to travel from the receiver to the satellite. This is because the receiver does not have precise knowledge of its own clock error, so it cannot accurately measure the round-trip time of the signal.



### Subsection 6.5b: Sources of Error in Pseudorange Measurements



As mentioned earlier, pseudorange measurements are affected by several sources of error. These errors can be categorized into two main types: systematic errors and random errors.



Systematic errors are caused by factors that affect all pseudorange measurements in a consistent manner. These errors can be caused by atmospheric delays, satellite clock errors, and receiver clock errors. Atmospheric delays occur when the GPS signal travels through the Earth's atmosphere, which can cause the signal to slow down or change direction. Satellite clock errors refer to inaccuracies in the satellite's onboard clock, which can affect the timing of the signal. Receiver clock errors, on the other hand, refer to inaccuracies in the receiver's clock, which can also affect the timing of the signal.



Random errors, on the other hand, are caused by factors that affect pseudorange measurements in an unpredictable manner. These errors can be caused by factors such as multipath interference, which occurs when the GPS signal reflects off of objects before reaching the receiver, causing a delay in the signal. Other sources of random errors include receiver noise and signal attenuation.



### Subsection 6.5c: Applications in Standard Positioning Service



Pseudorange measurements are used in the Standard Positioning Service (SPS) of the Global Positioning System (GPS). SPS is the civilian service provided by GPS, which is available to all users free of charge. It provides position, velocity, and timing information with an accuracy of about 10-20 meters.



In SPS, pseudorange measurements are used to calculate the receiver's position using a process called trilateration. Trilateration involves using the pseudorange measurements from at least three satellites to determine the receiver's position. By comparing the pseudorange measurements from multiple satellites, the receiver can determine its distance from each satellite and use this information to calculate its position.



Pseudorange measurements are also used in the SPS to provide timing information. The GPS system uses atomic clocks on board the satellites to provide precise timing information. By comparing the pseudorange measurements from multiple satellites, the receiver can determine the difference in time between the satellite's clock and its own clock, which can be used to synchronize the receiver's clock with the GPS system's clock.



In addition to SPS, pseudorange measurements are also used in other GPS services, such as the Precise Positioning Service (PPS) and the Differential GPS (DGPS) service. PPS is a military service that provides more accurate position, velocity, and timing information than SPS. DGPS is a service that uses ground-based reference stations to correct for errors in the GPS signal, resulting in more accurate position information.



Overall, pseudorange measurements play a crucial role in GPS navigation, providing accurate and reliable position, velocity, and timing information for a wide range of applications. As technology continues to advance, the use of pseudorange measurements in navigation systems will only continue to grow and improve.





# Modern Navigation: A Comprehensive Guide to Navigational Techniques and Systems



## Chapter 6: GPS Pseudo-range Measurements and Basic Principles



### Section 6.6: GPS Error Sources



In order to accurately determine a receiver's position using GPS, it is important to understand and account for the various sources of error that can affect the measurements. These errors can arise from a variety of factors, including atmospheric conditions, satellite and receiver clock errors, and technical malfunctions. In this section, we will discuss the different sources of error in GPS measurements and their impact on the accuracy of position calculations.



#### Subsection 6.6a: Satellite Clock Errors



One of the primary sources of error in GPS measurements is satellite clock errors. Each GPS satellite is equipped with an atomic clock that is used to accurately measure time. However, these clocks are not perfect and can experience errors due to factors such as temperature changes and aging. These errors can result in discrepancies in the time measurements, which in turn affect the accuracy of the pseudorange measurements.



To account for satellite clock errors, GPS receivers use a process called clock bias estimation. This involves comparing the time measurements from multiple satellites and calculating the difference between the expected and actual times. This difference, known as the clock bias, is then used to adjust the pseudorange measurements and improve the accuracy of the position calculation.



It is worth noting that satellite clock errors can also be caused by external factors such as solar flares and other space weather events. These events can disrupt the satellite's internal clock and introduce errors in the time measurements. To mitigate the impact of these events, GPS satellites are equipped with redundant clocks and monitoring systems to ensure accurate time measurements.



In addition to clock bias estimation, other techniques such as differential GPS (DGPS) and real-time kinematic (RTK) can also be used to correct for satellite clock errors. These techniques involve using a reference station with a known location to compare its time measurements with those of the GPS satellites and calculate the clock bias. This information is then transmitted to the receiver, allowing for more precise position calculations.



In conclusion, satellite clock errors are a significant source of error in GPS measurements. However, with the use of advanced techniques and redundant systems, these errors can be mitigated to ensure accurate and reliable navigation. 





#### Subsection 6.6b: Orbit Errors



Another significant source of error in GPS measurements is orbit errors. GPS satellites orbit the Earth at an altitude of approximately 20,000 km, and their positions are constantly changing due to gravitational forces from the Earth and other celestial bodies. These orbit errors can result in discrepancies in the satellite's expected position, which in turn affects the accuracy of the pseudorange measurements.



To account for orbit errors, GPS receivers use a process called orbit determination. This involves comparing the expected and actual positions of the satellites and calculating the difference between them. This difference, known as the orbit error, is then used to adjust the pseudorange measurements and improve the accuracy of the position calculation.



Orbit errors can also be caused by external factors such as atmospheric drag and solar radiation pressure. These factors can affect the satellite's orbit and introduce errors in the position measurements. To mitigate the impact of these factors, GPS satellites are equipped with thrusters and other systems to maintain their orbits and ensure accurate position measurements.



In addition to orbit determination, other techniques such as differential GPS (DGPS) and Kalman filtering can also be used to further improve the accuracy of GPS measurements by accounting for orbit errors. These techniques use a combination of satellite and ground-based measurements to estimate and correct for any errors in the satellite's orbit.



Overall, understanding and accounting for orbit errors is crucial in ensuring the accuracy of GPS measurements and the reliability of navigation systems that rely on them. With advancements in technology and techniques, the impact of orbit errors on GPS measurements continues to decrease, making GPS an increasingly reliable and precise navigation tool.





#### Subsection 6.6c: Atmospheric Delay Errors



In addition to orbit errors, another significant source of error in GPS measurements is atmospheric delay. As GPS signals travel through the Earth's atmosphere, they are affected by various atmospheric conditions such as temperature, pressure, and humidity. These conditions can cause the GPS signals to slow down or speed up, resulting in discrepancies in the pseudorange measurements.



The most significant contributor to atmospheric delay is the ionosphere, a layer of the Earth's atmosphere that contains charged particles. These charged particles can cause the GPS signals to bend and slow down, resulting in a longer travel time and an overestimation of the distance between the satellite and the receiver. This can lead to errors in the calculated position, especially in areas with high ionospheric activity.



To account for atmospheric delay, GPS receivers use a process called ionospheric correction. This involves measuring the total electron content (TEC) of the ionosphere and using mathematical models to estimate the delay caused by the ionosphere. This correction is then applied to the pseudorange measurements to improve the accuracy of the position calculation.



Another source of atmospheric delay is the troposphere, the lowest layer of the Earth's atmosphere. The troposphere is affected by weather conditions such as temperature, pressure, and humidity, which can cause the GPS signals to slow down or speed up. This can result in errors in the pseudorange measurements, especially in areas with extreme weather conditions.



To account for tropospheric delay, GPS receivers use a process called tropospheric correction. This involves measuring the temperature, pressure, and humidity of the troposphere and using mathematical models to estimate the delay caused by these factors. This correction is then applied to the pseudorange measurements to improve the accuracy of the position calculation.



In addition to these corrections, other techniques such as differential GPS (DGPS) and Kalman filtering can also be used to further improve the accuracy of GPS measurements by accounting for atmospheric delay. These techniques use a combination of satellite and ground-based measurements to estimate and correct for any errors caused by atmospheric conditions.



Overall, understanding and accounting for atmospheric delay is crucial in ensuring the accuracy of GPS measurements and the reliability of navigation systems that rely on them. With advancements in technology and techniques, the impact of atmospheric delay on GPS measurements continues to decrease, making GPS an increasingly reliable and precise navigation tool.





#### Subsection 6.6d: Receiver Errors



In addition to orbit errors and atmospheric delay, another source of error in GPS measurements is receiver errors. These errors can occur due to various factors such as hardware limitations, signal processing errors, and antenna placement.



One common type of receiver error is clock bias. GPS receivers use an internal clock to measure the time it takes for a signal to travel from a satellite to the receiver. However, these clocks are not perfect and can have slight discrepancies, resulting in errors in the pseudorange measurements. To account for this, GPS receivers use a process called clock bias correction, which involves comparing the receiver's clock to the atomic clocks on the GPS satellites and adjusting the measurements accordingly.



Another source of receiver error is multipath interference. This occurs when the GPS signal reflects off of nearby objects before reaching the receiver, resulting in a longer travel time and an overestimation of the distance between the satellite and the receiver. This can be caused by buildings, trees, or other structures in the surrounding environment. To mitigate this error, GPS receivers use advanced signal processing techniques to filter out these reflected signals and only use the direct signal from the satellite.



Antenna placement can also affect the accuracy of GPS measurements. If the antenna is not placed in an optimal location, it can result in signal blockage or interference, leading to errors in the pseudorange measurements. To minimize this error, GPS receivers use multiple antennas and advanced algorithms to combine the signals and improve the accuracy of the measurements.



Lastly, hardware limitations can also contribute to receiver errors. Older GPS receivers may not have the same capabilities as newer ones, resulting in less accurate measurements. Additionally, factors such as temperature and power supply can also affect the performance of the receiver and introduce errors in the measurements.



To account for receiver errors, GPS receivers use a process called receiver autonomous integrity monitoring (RAIM). This involves comparing the measurements from multiple satellites and identifying any outliers or discrepancies. If a significant error is detected, the receiver can discard that measurement and use the remaining ones to calculate a more accurate position.



In conclusion, receiver errors can significantly impact the accuracy of GPS measurements. However, with advancements in technology and the use of various correction techniques, these errors can be minimized, allowing for more precise navigation and positioning. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the basic principles of GPS pseudo-range measurements and how they are used in modern navigation systems. We have learned about the concept of pseudo-range, which is the distance between a satellite and a receiver, and how it is calculated using the time difference between the transmission and reception of a signal. We have also discussed the importance of accurate clock synchronization and the role of the ionosphere and troposphere in affecting the signal's travel time.



Furthermore, we have delved into the concept of dilution of precision (DOP) and how it affects the accuracy of GPS measurements. We have seen that DOP is influenced by the geometry of the satellites in view and how a higher DOP value can result in a less accurate position fix. We have also explored the different types of DOP and their significance in navigation.



Lastly, we have discussed the limitations of GPS and how it can be affected by factors such as signal blockage, multipath interference, and intentional jamming. We have also briefly touched upon the advancements in GPS technology, such as the use of multiple frequencies and differential GPS, to improve accuracy and reliability.



Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive understanding of GPS pseudo-range measurements and their role in modern navigation. By mastering these concepts, readers will be equipped with the necessary knowledge to effectively use GPS in their navigation endeavors.

In this chapter, we will delve into the effects of the Earth's atmosphere on navigation and how it can cause delays in signals. We will also explore the ionosphere, a layer of the Earth's atmosphere that is ionized by solar radiation, and its impact on navigation. We will discuss the challenges that these effects pose to navigation and the methods used to correct for them.
